The Ash'ari Definition of Sustenance and Mu'tazilite Objections
The author presents the Ash'ari definition of sustenance and the Mu'tazilite objections against it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of sustenance (rizq) and how it differs from the Mu'tazilite definitions, along with the key objections.

Core Idea
Sustenance is whatever God provides for benefit, whether lawful or unlawful.

This lesson examines the theological debate over the definition of sustenance. The Ash'arites define it broadly as anything beneficial that God provides, while the Mu'tazilites restrict it to lawful provisions or provisions not prevented from benefiting. We'll see the arguments and counterarguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Get a sense of the two positions.
Notice the author's comment that the Ash'ari definition is not a strict definition but a negation.
2
Identify the Ash'ari definition
Find what the Ash'arites say sustenance is. Note the two versions and al-Amidi's preference.
The definition includes both lawful and unlawful because nothing from God is ugly.
3
Examine the Mu'tazilite definitions
Identify the two Mu'tazilite definitions and the objections raised against them using Quranic verses.
First definition is refuted by animal sustenance; second leads to problematic consequences.
4
Understand the spending objection and answer
Read how the objection about spending from sustenance is answered by invoking metaphorical usage.
This is a key point in defending the Ash'ari view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : الرزق وهو عندنا كل ما ساقه الله إلى العبد فأكله فهو رزق له الله حلالاً كان أو حراماً إذ لا يقبح من الله شيء ليس ما ذكره تحديداً للرزق بل هو نفي لما ادعى عن تخصيصه بالحلال، وذلك لأن مذهب الأشاعرة أن الرزق كل ما انتفع به حي سواء كان بالتغذي أو بغيره مباحاً كان أو حراماً وربما قال بعضهم : هو كل ما يتربى به الحيوانات من الأغذية والأشربة لا غير، قال الآمدي : والتعويل على الأول فإن قيل : كيف يتصور لا إنفاق من الرزق بالمعنى الثاني الذي ذهب إليه بعضهم وقد قال تعالى : ومما رزقناهم ينفقون ﴾ [البقرة :٣]، أجيب بأن إطلاق الرزق على المنفق مجاز عندهم لأنه بصدده ( وأما هم ) أي المعتزلة هو ( ففسروه بالحلال تارة فأورد عليهم وما من دابة في الأرض إلا على الله رزقها [ هود : ٦] فللبهائم رزق ولا يتصور في حقها ولا حرمة ، (و) فسروره أخرى ( بما لا يمنع من الانتفاع به أخرى فيلزمهم أن من أكل الحرام طول عمره فالله لم يرزقه وهو
Fourth: Sustenance (al-rizq). And it, according to us, is everything that God brings to the servant and he eats it, so it is sustenance for him from God, whether it is lawful or unlawful, since nothing is ugly from God. It is not that he mentioned a definition for sustenance, rather it is a negation of the claim of its restriction to the lawful. And that is because the school of the Ash'arites is that sustenance is everything from which a living being benefits, whether by nourishment or otherwise, whether permissible or unlawful. And perhaps some of them said: It is everything by which animals are nourished from foods and drinks only. Al-Amidi said: Reliance is on the first. If it is said: How is it conceivable to spend from sustenance according to the second meaning that some hold, while He says: 'And from what We have provided them they spend' (Qur'an 2:3)? It is answered that the application of 'sustenance' to what is spent is metaphorical according to them, because it is in its context. As for them, i.e., the Mu'tazilites, they interpreted it sometimes as the lawful, and it was objected to them: 'And there is no creature on earth but that upon God is its sustenance' (Qur'an 11:6). So animals have sustenance, and in their case neither lawfulness nor unlawfulness is conceivable. And they interpreted it another time as that which does not prevent benefiting from it another time, so it follows that whoever eats the unlawful for his entire life, God has not provided him, and he...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage defines sustenance (al-rizq) according to the Ash'arites and presents the Mu'tazilite definitions with objections. The author states the Ash'ari view: sustenance is everything that God brings to a servant and he eats, whether lawful (ḥalāl) or unlawful (ḥarām), because nothing is ugly from God. This is not a strict definition but a negation of restricting sustenance to the lawful. The Ash'ari school holds that sustenance is anything from which a living being benefits, whether by feeding or otherwise, permissible or not. Some said it is only foods and drinks. Al-Amidi says the first view is relied upon. The objection about spending from sustenance in the second view is answered by saying that the term 'sustenance' is applied metaphorically to what is spent. The Mu'tazilites defined sustenance in two ways: (1) as the lawful, but this is refuted by the verse that God provides for all creatures, including animals, where lawfulness does not apply. (2) as that which does not prevent benefiting from it, but this leads to the problem that someone who eats only unlawful food his whole life would not have been provided by God.

Key Terms
الرزق (al-rizq)
الحلال (al-ḥalāl)
الحرام (al-ḥarām)
الانتفاع (al-intifāʿ)
التغذي (al-taghaddhī)
المباح (al-mubāḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari definition of sustenance is a positive definition that lists all types of sustenance.
Correction: The passage states it is a negation of restricting sustenance to the lawful, not an exhaustive definition.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'arites, which of the following is considered sustenance (rizq)?
A) Only lawful food
B) Anything from which a living being benefits, whether lawful or unlawful
C) Only what is consumed by feeding
D) Only what is given as charity

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari definition of sustenance includes all beneficial provisions from God, regardless of legal status, countering the Mu'tazilite view that restricts it to the lawful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you recognize that everything you consume, whether lawful or unlawful, is ultimately provided by God? How does this awareness change your relationship with Him and your struggle with sin? Will you thank Him for the lawful and repent from the unlawful, knowing He is the source of all?
?
If you believe that nothing from God is ugly, how do you respond when He provides you with hardship or test through your provisions? Can you genuinely say that even the haram you ate was part of His decree? How does this truth call you to deeper trust and humility?
?
The Mu'tazilites wanted to restrict sustenance to the lawful to defend God's justice. But the Ash'aris say God is just even when He provides the unlawful. Which view leads you to greater awe of God's sovereignty? And how does this affect your daily attitude toward the food on your plate?

The Definition of Provision (Rizq) According to the Ash'aris
Al-Amidi states the Ash'ari definition of rizq and refutes Mu'tazilite restrictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of provision (rizq) and how it refutes the Mu'tazilite restriction to halal, while also seeing the connection to the principle of rational good and evil.

Core Idea
Provision is whatever Allah gives, whether halal or haram.

This lesson delves into one of the key theological disputes between the Ash'aris and Mu'tazilites: the definition of divine provision (rizq). Al-Amidi presents the Ash'ari position that provision includes both permissible and forbidden sustenance, challenging the Mu'tazilite view that restricts it to halal. As you read, pay attention to how the definition is defended and how it ties into broader debates about God's actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English versions to get a feel for the argument. Notice the flow from definition to objection to refutation.
2
Identify the Ash'ari definition and its rationale
Find the core Ash'ari definition: provision is everything Allah brings and the servant eats, whether halal or haram. Note the rationale: nothing from Allah is ugly.
This negates the claim that provision is only halal.
3
Recognize the two Mu'tazilite definitions and their refutations
The Mu'tazilites define provision as only halal, or as that which does not prevent benefit. The refutations use Quranic verses and consensus.
Note how each definition leads to absurd consequences.
4
Connect to the underlying principle
The refutation reveals the corruption of the Mu'tazilite principle of rational good/evil. Understand that the falsity of the branches proves the root false.
This is a common pattern in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : الرزق وهو عندنا كل ما ساقه الله إلى العبد فأكله فهو رزق له الله حلالاً كان أو حراماً إذ لا يقبح من الله شيء ليس ما ذكره تحديداً للرزق بل هو نفي لما ادعى عن تخصيصه بالحلال، وذلك لأن مذهب الأشاعرة أن الرزق كل ما انتفع به حي سواء كان بالتغذي أو بغيره مباحاً كان أو حراماً وربما قال بعضهم : هو كل ما يتربى به الحيوانات من الأغذية والأشربة لا غير، قال الآمدي : والتعويل على الأول فإن قيل : كيف يتصور لا إنفاق من الرزق بالمعنى الثاني الذي ذهب إليه بعضهم وقد قال تعالى : ومما رزقناهم ينفقون ﴾ [البقرة :٣]، أجيب بأن إطلاق الرزق على المنفق مجاز عندهم لأنه بصدده ( وأما هم ) أي المعتزلة ( ففسروه بالحلال تارة فأورد عليهم وما من دابة في الأرض إلا على الله رزقها [ هود : ٦] فللبهائم رزق ولا يتصور في حقها ولا حرمة ، (و) فسروره أخرى ( بما لا يمنع من الانتفاع به أخرى فيلزمهم أن من أكل الحرام طول عمره فالله لم يرزقه وهو خلاف الإجماع) من الأمة قبل ظهور المعتزلة ( كل ذلك ) الذي يرد عليهم ويلزمهم ناع عليهم فساد أصلهم في الحكم على الله بيجوز ولا يجوز )، وذلك الأصل هو قاعدة السن والقبح العقليين فإنها منشأ لأباطيل كثيرة متفرعة عليها وبطلان الفروع اللازمة شاهد صدق على بطلان أصلها
The fourth: provision (al-rizq), and it is with us: everything that Allah brings to the servant and he eats it, so it is provision for him, whether it is halal or haram, because nothing is ugly from Allah. What he mentioned is not a definition of provision, but rather a negation of what was claimed about its restriction to halal. That is because the school of the Ash'aris is that provision is everything from which a living being benefits, whether by feeding or otherwise, permissible or forbidden. And perhaps some of them said: it is only what animals are nourished with from foods and drinks. Al-Amidi said: reliance is on the first. For if it is said: how can spending from provision be imagined according to the second meaning which some held, when He said: 'And from what We have provided them, they spend' (2:3)? It is answered that applying the term 'provision' to what is spent is metaphorical for them because it is intended for it. (As for them) i.e. the Mu'tazila, (they interpreted it as halal sometimes, so it was objected to them: 'And there is no creature on earth except that its provision is upon Allah' (11:6); so there is provision for beasts, and in their case neither halal nor haram is conceivable. And they interpreted it another time as that which does not prevent benefiting from it, so it follows that one who eats haram his entire life, Allah has not provided for him, which is contrary to consensus) of the community before the emergence of the Mu'tazila. (All that) which is objected to them and follows from them, they criticize the corruption of their principle in judging Allah regarding what is permissible and impermissible. And that principle is the basis of the rational good and evil, for it is the source of many false branches derived from it, and the falsity of the necessary branches is a true witness to the falsity of their principle.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of provision (rizq) according to the Ash'ari school and refutes the Mu'tazilite positions. The author first gives the Ash'ari definition: provision is everything Allah brings to a servant and he eats, whether halal or haram. He clarifies that this is not a strict definition but a negation of the claim that provision is only halal. He then explains the broader Ash'ari view: provision is anything from which a living being benefits, by food or otherwise, permissible or forbidden. Some limit it to nourishments. Al-Amidi says reliance is on the first, broader view. An objection is raised: how can spending (infaq) be from provision if provision is only what is eaten? The answer is that 'provision' is applied metaphorically to what is spent because it is intended for that purpose. Next, the Mu'tazilite views are presented: they sometimes define provision as halal, which is refuted by the verse that Allah provides for beasts, for which halal/haram is irrelevant. Another Mu'tazilite definition is 'what does not prevent benefiting from it', but that leads to the conclusion that a lifelong haram-eater has no provision from Allah, which contradicts consensus (ijma'). This shows the corruption of their principle of rational good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqliyyān), which generates false branches; the falsity of the branches proves the falsity of the root.

الحلال
الحرام
المعتزلة
الأشاعرة
الحسن والقبح العقليين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Provision (rizq) consists only of halal food and drink.
Correction: According to the Ash'aris, provision includes both halal and haram because it is whatever Allah provides, and nothing from Allah is inherently ugly or evil. The Mu'tazilite restriction is refuted by scriptural evidence and consensus.

Checkpoint

B) Yes, because provision is anything from which a living being benefits, regardless of legal status.
C) Only if it is eaten unintentionally.
D) Only for animals, not humans.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari definition of provision is comprehensive, including both halal and haram, challenging the Mu'tazilite restriction and exposing the flaw in their rationalist principle.

?

?
This lesson shows that a false principle (rational good and evil) leads to false conclusions. What core beliefs do you hold about Allah that might be built on human reasoning rather than revelation? Will you examine them in light of this passage?

God Wills All Existents: Collective vs Detailed Attribution
Al-Amidi affirms Allah's will for all existents and discusses permissibility of detailed attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position on divine will, including why attributing will to kufr and fisq in detail is impermissible.

Core Idea
Allah wills everything that happens, but we do not say He wills evil in detail.

The Ash'ari school affirms that Allah wills all existents—whatever occurs—and does not will what does not occur. However, this teaching comes with a crucial nuance about how we speak of divine will. In this passage, al-Amidi explains the general and detailed attribution of will to created events, and why detailed statements about evil require caution.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the distinction between collective and detailed attribution.
2
Identify the two levels of attribution
Find where the passage discusses attributing will collectively ('جميع الكائنات') and then discusses the prohibition of detailed attribution ('لا يقال: الكفر أو الفسق مراد لله').
This distinction is central to the lesson.
3
Understand the reason and analogy
Note why detailed attribution is problematic: it implies command. Compare with the creation analogy: we say 'Creator of everything' but not 'Creator of filth' in detail.
The analogy reinforces the principle of safeguarding divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات ] ( أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات غير مريد لما لا يكون ) فكل كائن مراد له وما ليس بكائن ليس بمراد له ( هذا مذهب أهل الحق ) واتفقوا على جواز إسناد الكل إليه جملة، فيقال: جميع الكائنات مرادة لله تعالى ( لكن اختلفوا في التفصيل لا يجوز إسناد الكائنات إليه مفصلاً فلا يقال : الكفر أو الفسق مراد لله منهم من تعالى ( لإيهامه الكفر، وهو أن الكفر أو الفسق مأمور به لما ذهب إليه بعض العلماء أن الأمر هو نفس الإرادة ) وعند الإلباس يجب التوقف عن الإطلاق إلى التوقيف )، من والإعلام من الشارع ) ولا توقيف ثمة) أي في الإسناد تفصيلا ( وذلك ) الذي ذكرناه صحة الإطلاق إجمالاً لا تفصيلاً، كما يصح ) بالإجماع، والنص ( أن يقال: الله خالق كل شيء ولا يصح أن يقال : إنه خالق القاذورات وخالق القردة والخنازير) مع
The fourth aim: that He, exalted, is the willer of all existents. (That He, exalted, is the willer of all existents, not willing what does not occur). So every existent is willed by Him, and what is not existent is not willed by Him. (This is the doctrine of the people of truth). And they have agreed on the permissibility of attributing all things to Him collectively; thus it is said: all existents are willed by Allah Almighty. (But they differed in detail: it is not permissible to attribute existents to Him in detail, so it is not said: kufr or fisq is willed by Allah) because that implies kufr (and that is that kufr or fisq is commanded, according to some scholars who hold that command is the same as will). And when ambiguity occurs, it is obligatory to refrain from absolute statements until (the specification which is) the notification from the Lawgiver. And there is no specification there, i.e., in detailed attribution. (And that) which we have mentioned is the correctness of attribution generally, not in detail, just as it is correct, by consensus and text, to say: Allah is the Creator of everything, but it is not correct to say: He is the Creator of filth and the creator of monkeys and pigs.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage states the Ash'ari position on divine will (irāda). It asserts that Allah wills all existents (those that occur) and does not will what does not occur. This is the doctrine of 'people of truth' (ahl al-haqq). They agree that one can say collectively 'all existents are willed by Allah'. However, they differ on detailed attribution: it is not permissible to say 'kufr or fisq is willed by Allah' because that might imply that Allah commands them, if one equates command with will (as some scholars do). Therefore, to avoid such misimpression, when there is ambiguity, one must refrain from such statements unless the Lawgiver (al-shāri') explicitly specifies. Since no such specification exists for detailed attribution, it is not said. The author compares this to saying 'Allah is the Creator of everything' but not 'Creator of filth or monkeys/pigs' in detail, even though in general He creates all. The point is to protect the understanding of divine will from implying approval of evil.

Key Terms
المريد
الكائنات
الإرادة
الكفر
الفسق
الأمر
التوقيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris believe that Allah wills evil acts in the same way He wills good acts, implying approval or command.
Correction: Ash'aris only affirm that Allah wills evil acts as occurrences, but they prohibit detailed attribution to avoid implying divine command or approval. They maintain a distinction between will (irāda) and command (amr).

Checkpoint
Why is it not permissible to say in detail that 'kufr is willed by Allah' according to this passage?
A) Because Allah does not will kufr at all.
B) Because it might imply that kufr is commanded.
C) Because the Mu'tazila forbid it.
D) Because there is a consensus that kufr is not willed.

Key Takeaway
Allah wills all occurrences, but detailed attribution of evil acts is prohibited to avoid implying divine command or approval.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own sins, do you find yourself excusing them by simply saying 'Allah willed it' in detail, thereby avoiding personal responsibility? How does this lesson's distinction between collective and detailed attribution call you to take ownership of your actions while still affirming Allah's overarching will?
?
How often do you speak about difficult topics like evil or suffering in a way that might imply Allah approves of them? What changes in your speech can you make today to ensure you never attribute evil to Allah in a way that disrespects His holiness?
?
If you truly believed that detailed attribution of will to evil acts could lead to a misunderstanding of Allah's perfect justice, would you be willing to guard your tongue even when you are angry or frustrated? Is there a specific situation you are currently facing where you need to apply this principle?

God's Will: General and Specific Attribution
Defines God's will for all things, general vs. specific attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Haqq distinguishes between general and specific attribution of God's will, and the rationale behind avoiding detailed attribution of sin.

Core Idea
God wills all that occurs, but not all that is willed is approved—avoiding misunderstanding requires careful language.

This passage from al-Mawaqif explains the orthodox position on God's will: He wills all contingent things, but there is a crucial distinction between general and specific attribution. While we can say 'all things are willed by God' in general, we cannot say 'God wills disbelief' in detail, because that could imply divine approval of sin. The author provides analogies from creation and ownership to illustrate the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the main claim: God wills all that occurs, not what does not occur.
The passage begins with a clear doctrinal statement.
2
Identify the distinction
Look for the difference between general attribution (saying 'all things are willed by God') and specific attribution (saying 'God wills disbelief'). Why is the latter problematic?
The reason given is that it suggests disbelief is commanded.
3
Examine the analogies
The author gives two analogies: creation (God creates everything, but we don't say He creates filth) and ownership (everything belongs to Him, but we don't say He has spouses). How do these support the rule?
Both analogies show that general statements are fine, but specific ones can be misleading.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع : أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات ]
( أنه تعالى مريد لجميع الكائنات غير مريد لما لا يكون ) فكل كائن مراد له وما ليس بكائن ليس بمراد له ( هذا مذهب أهل الحق ) واتفقوا على جواز إسناد الكل إليه جملة، فيقال: جميع الكائنات مرادة لله تعالى ( لكن اختلفوا في التفصيل لا يجوز إسناد الكائنات إليه مفصلاً فلا يقال : الكفر أو الفسق مراد لله منهم من تعالى ( لإيهامه الكفر، وهو أن الكفر أو الفسق مأمور به لما ذهب إليه بعض العلماء أن الأمر هو نفس الإرادة ) وعند الإلباس يجب التوقف عن الإطلاق إلى التوقيف )، من والإعلام من الشارع ) ولا توقيف ثمة) أي في الإسناد تفصيلا ( وذلك ) الذي ذكرناه صحة الإطلاق إجمالاً لا تفصيلاً، كما يصح ) بالإجماع، والنص ( أن يقال: الله خالق كل شيء ولا يصح أن يقال : إنه خالق القاذورات وخالق القردة والخنازير) مع كونها مخلوقة له اتفاقاً، وكما يقال : له كل ما في السموات والأرض) أي هو مالكها، ) ولا يقال : له الزوجات والأولاد لإيهامه إضافة غير الملك إِليه ، ومنهم من جوز أن قال : الله مريد للكفر والفسق والمعصية معاقباً عليها .
قوله : ( لإيهامه إضافة غير الملك أي اضافة الزوجات والأولاد من حيث أنها زوجات و أولاد لا إضافة الملك .
The fourth goal: That He, exalted, wills all contingent things.
(He, exalted, wills all contingent things and does not will what is not.) So every occurring thing is willed by Him, and what does not occur is not willed by Him. (This is the doctrine of the people of truth.) And they agreed on the permissibility of attributing all to Him in general, so it is said: All contingent things are willed by God, exalted. (But they differed regarding detail: it is not permissible to attribute contingent things to Him in detail, so one does not say: Disbelief or sin is willed by God—some of them [said]—because it suggests disbelief, i.e., that disbelief or sin is commanded, due to the view of some scholars that command is identical to will.) And when there is ambiguity, one must refrain from absolute (attribution) until (there is) specification and notification from the Lawgiver. And there is no specification there, i.e., in detailed attribution. (And that) which we have mentioned is the correctness of attribution in general, not in detail, just as it is correct by consensus and text to say: God is the Creator of everything, but it is not correct to say: He is the Creator of filth, monkeys, and pigs, despite their being created by Him by agreement. And just as it is said: To Him belongs everything in the heavens and the earth, i.e., He is their Owner, but it is not said: To Him belong spouses and children, because it suggests an attribution other than ownership. And some have permitted saying: God wills disbelief, sin, and disobedience while punishing them.
His saying: (because it suggests an attribution other than ownership) i.e., the attribution of spouses and children insofar as they are spouses and children, not the attribution of ownership.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines the core doctrine of God's will according to Ahl al-Haqq. It begins by stating that God wills all that occurs and does not will what does not occur. Then it explains that all scholars agree on the permissibility of general attribution (e.g., 'all things are willed by God'), but they differ on detailed attribution: it is not allowed to say specifically that disbelief or sin is willed by God because that might imply that such acts are commanded (since some equate command with will). In cases of ambiguity, one must stop until guidance from the Lawgiver is found. The commentator gives analogies: it is correct to say 'God is the Creator of everything' but not 'He is the Creator of filth, monkeys, and pigs' (though they are created), and it is correct to say 'everything in the heavens and earth belongs to Him' as ownership, but not 'He has spouses and children' because that implies a relationship other than ownership. He notes that some scholars do permit saying that God wills disbelief and sin while punishing them.

Key Terms
المراد
الإسناد إجمالاً وتفصيلاً
الإلباس
التوقيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will for all things means He approves of sin.
Correction: The passage distinguishes general will (all things occur by His will) from specific attribution (which might imply command). So God can will something without approving of it.

Checkpoint
Why is it not allowed to say 'God wills disbelief' in detail according to the passage?
A) Because disbelief is not willed.
B) Because it might imply disbelief is commanded.
C) Because it is not mentioned in the text.
D) Because the Mu'tazila forbid it.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Haqq affirm that God wills all things, but to avoid misunderstanding, detailed attribution of sin to God's will is avoided, while general attribution is permitted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider a past sin that you committed, do you admit that God willed it (in general) while also taking full responsibility for your choice? Or do you silently blame Him? How can you reconcile His will with your accountability starting today?
?
Think of a time you casually said something like 'God made me do it' or 'It was God's will that I sinned.' How does this passage's warning against detailed attribution convict you? What specific change will you make in your speech about God's will?
?
The scholars refrained from attributing filth, monkeys, and pigs to God as Creator in detail, even though He created them. Are there things in your life that you need to 'refrain from attributing to God'—not denying His sovereignty, but avoiding careless words that might imply approval? What one phrase will you stop using today?

The Mu'tazila View on God's Will and Its Critique
Presents Mu'tazila view and critique via analogy argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position on God's will regarding human actions and the critique that this implies most human acts oppose God's will.

Core Idea
Restricting God's will to obedience makes most events contrary to His will.

This lesson presents the Mu'tazila view that God wills only commanded acts from humans and dislikes sins. We will then examine a powerful critique: if God dislikes most human actions, then He is perpetually displeased, which seems problematic. The passage also discusses the validity of analogical reasoning from the seen to the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila statement
Read the first part of the passage where the Mu'tazila position is stated. Note that they distinguish between God's own acts and human acts.
Focus on the classification of human acts into categories.
2
Identify the categories
The passage lists five categories of human acts: obligatory, forbidden, recommended, discouraged, and permissible. For each, note what God wills or dislikes.
3
Understand the critique
Read the critique from Sharh al-Maqasid: if God dislikes all sins, then most human acts are against His will, implying no one is truly obedient.
Consider the rhetorical point about a village chief.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The commentator argues that analogizing God's attributes to human attributes (patience, forbearance) is invalid. God's patience is infinite.
Note the distinction between God's absolute will and His will as a test.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت المعتزلة : هو مريد لجميع أفعاله غير إرادته الحادثة عند من أثبتها وأما افعال العباد فهو مريد للمأمور به، منها كاره للمعاصي والكفر وتفصيله أن فعل العبد إن كان واجباً يريد الله وقوعه ويكره تركه وإن كان حراماً فبعكسه والمندوب يريد وقوعه ولا يكره تركه والمكروه عكسه، وأما المباح وأفعال غير المكلف فلا يتعلق بها إرادة ولا كراهة لنا.
قوله : ( عند من أثبتها) وهم معتزلة البصرة كما ذكرناه في بحث الإرادة .
قوله : ( كاره للمعاصي والكفر قال في شرح المقاصد : يلزم من هذا أن يكون أكثر ما يقع من العباد خلاف مراده تعالى والظاهر أنه لا يصير على هذا رئيس قرية من عباده، وأنت خبير بأن الظاهر أنه لا يصير على خلاف رضا أيضاً مع أن أهل السنة جوزوه في حق الباري تعالى فالحق أن قياس الغائب على الشاهد فيما ذكر مما لا وجه له كيف له كيف وصبر الله تعالى وحلمه بحر لا نهاية له فكيف يقاس في السعة والإحاطة على صبر عباده الذي هو بمنزلة القطرة منه، نعم ما أراده الله تعالى إرادة تامة لا يتخلف عنها، وأما ما أراده أن يفعله العبد برغبته واختياره ابتلاء له ففي عدم جواز تخلفه بحث ظاهر
And the Mu'tazila said: He wills all His acts, except His originated will according to those who affirm it. As for the acts of servants, He wills that which is commanded, and among them He dislikes acts of disobedience and disbelief. Its detail is that if the servant's act is obligatory, God wills its occurrence and dislikes its omission; if it is forbidden, the opposite; the recommended: He wills its occurrence and does not dislike its omission; the discouraged: the opposite; as for permissible acts and acts of the non-mukallaf, no will or dislike is attached to them in our view.

His saying: (according to those who affirm it) i.e., the Basran Mu'tazila, as we mentioned in the discussion on will.

His saying: (He dislikes acts of disobedience and disbelief). He said in Sharh al-Maqasid: It follows from this that most of what occurs from servants is contrary to His will. And it is apparent that on this (view), no chief of a village would be from among His servants. And you are well aware that it is apparent that it does not come contrary to approval either, while Ahl al-Sunna have permitted that in the case of God, exalted. So the truth is that the analogy of the unseen from the witnessed in what is mentioned has no basis. How can it be? And the patience and forbearance of God are an infinite sea, so how can it be measured in capacity and encompassment against the patience of His servants, which is like a drop from it? Yes, what God wills with a complete will never fails to occur. But as for what He intends that the servant does willingly and by choice as a test, there is clear discussion concerning its inability to fail.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila view on God's will. They hold that God wills only His own acts (excluding a created will, according to some). For human acts, He wills only what is commanded (obedience) and dislikes sins and disbelief. They detail categories: for obligatory, He wills its occurrence and dislikes its omission; for forbidden, the opposite; for recommended, He wills occurrence but does not dislike omission; for discouraged, the opposite; for permissible and acts of non-mukallaf, no will or dislike. The commentator then critiques this view from Sharh al-Maqasid: it implies that most human acts are against God's will, which would mean no one is obedient (no chief of a village is from His servants). Also, even though Ahl al-Sunna allow that God's approval can be violated, the analogy from the seen to the unseen is invalid because God's attributes are incomparable. God's patience is infinite. The commentator distinguishes between God's absolute will (which never fails) and God's desire that the servant act willingly as a test (whether that can fail is debatable).

Key Terms
المأمور به
المعاصي
الكفر
قياس الغائب على الشاهد
الصبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila view makes God's will contingent on human choice, but they aim to preserve God from willing evil.
Correction: The Mu'tazila restrict God's will to obedience to avoid attributing evil to Him, but critics argue this leads to the absurdity that most events oppose God's will.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what does God will regarding sinful acts?
A) He wills them.
B) He dislikes them.
C) He neither wills nor dislikes.
D) He wills them but punishes.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view that God only wills obedience leads to the problematic conclusion that most human acts oppose His will, exposing the limits of analogical reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, what do you truly believe God wills for you at that moment? Does the Mu'tazila view make you more aware that even your 'permissible' actions are areas where you might be ignoring God's will? How will you adjust your daily choices to align more with what God commands, knowing His patience is infinite?
?
The commentator says God's patience is like an infinite sea compared to a drop. When you think of your repeated failures, do you rely on that patience as a license to continue sinning, or as a call to deeper gratitude? What is one area of your life where you are testing God's patience right now, and what will you change today?
?
If most of your actions are contrary to God's will in the Mu'tazila sense, how would that change your sense of accountability? Are you willing to examine your daily routine and categorize your actions as commanded, forbidden, or permissible? Write down one action you thought was neutral but might actually be an area where you are not seeking God's will.

Two Proofs for Allah's Universal Will: Creation and Specification
Two rational proofs for Allah's universal will, with commentary addressing objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs that Allah wills all things: the proof from creation without coercion and the proof from the need for a specifying attribute for possibles.

Core Idea
Allah's will is proven both by His free creation and by the necessary specification of possibles.

The Ash'ari doctrine holds that Allah is the willer of all things. This lesson presents two foundational proofs: first, that the Creator without coercion must will His creation; second, that since all possibles are equally within His power, the fact that some occur at specific times requires a specifying attribute, which is will. The passage also addresses key objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two proofs
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author presents two distinct arguments for the same conclusion: Allah's will encompasses all things.
The first proof is from creation, the second from specification.
2
Analyze the first proof: creation without coercion
The first proof argues that because Allah creates all things without coercion, He must will them. Examine the objection that absolute creation does not imply will, and the answer that creation by power suffices.
3
Analyze the second proof: specification requires a specifier
The second proof argues that since all possibles are equally within Allah's power, their occurrence at specific times requires a specifying attribute—will. Note the objection about will only applying to one's own actions and the response.
4
Reflect on the relationship between the proofs
Notice that the first proof, when properly understood, reduces to the second. Also, note how the commentary clarifies the significance of 'without coercion' and 'by power'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما أنه مريد للكائنات بأسرها فلانه خالق الأشياء كلها لما مر من جميع الحوادث إلى قدرته تعالى ابتداء وخالق الشيء بلا إكراه مريداً له بالضرورة وأيضاً قد ثبت أن جميع الممكنات مقدورة لله تعالى فلا بد في اختصاص بعضها بالوقوع وبأوقاتها المخصوصة من مخصص وهو الإرادة وهذا معنى قوله فالصفة المرجحة لأحد المقدورين هو الإرادة كما مر ولا بد منها أي من الصفة المرجحة في إيجاد بعض المقدورات دون بعض وفي تخصيص الموجودات بأوقاتها. قوله : ( وخالق الشيء بلا إكراه مريد له فيه بحث لأن الخلق المطلق لا يدل على الإرادة لاحتمال الإيجاب والخلق بالقدرة يدل لكن يؤول هذا الدليل حينئذ إلى الدليل الثاني وأيضاً يكون قوله : بلا إكراه مستدركاً، والجواب أن المراد الحق بالقدرة لكن نفي الإكراه بعد ثبوت القدرة يكفي في إثبات الإرادة، ولا يلاحظ هاهنا أن مخصص بعض المقدورات بالوقوع هو الإرادة بخلاف الوجه الثاني فإن مثبت الإرادة فيه هو هذا. قوله : (قد ثبت أن جميع الممكنات مقدورة لله تعالى فائدة إلحاق هذا الكلام دفع ما يقال : من أن الإرادة مرجحة في فعل المريد لا في فعل الغير فعلى تقدير كون أفعال العباد مخلوقة لهم لا يحتاج إلى إرادته تعالى، أعني التخصيص فيها .
As for that He is willing for all things, it is because He is the creator of all things, as all events are traced back to His power from the beginning, and the creator of a thing without coercion is necessarily willing it. Also, it has been established that all possibles are within God's power, so there must be in the specification of some of them for occurrence and for their specific times a specifying attribute, which is will. This is the meaning of His saying: 'The specifying attribute for one of two possible things is will, as has passed, and it is necessary' i.e., the specifying attribute is necessary in bringing about some possibles rather than others and in specifying existents for their times. His statement: 'And the creator of a thing without coercion is willing' — there is discussion in it, because absolute creation does not indicate will due to the possibility of necessitation, while creation by power does indicate, but then this proof reduces to the second proof, and also his saying 'without coercion' becomes redundant. The answer is that what is meant is the truth by power, but negation of coercion after establishing power suffices to prove will, and it is not considered here that the specifier of some possibles for occurrence is will, unlike the second aspect, for the establisher of will in it is this. His statement: 'It has been established that all possibles are within God's power' — the benefit of attaching this speech is to repel what is said: that will is a specifying factor in the action of the willing agent, not in the action of another, so on the assumption that the acts of servants are created by them, they do not need His will, I mean specification in them.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the Ash'ari doctrine that Allah is the willer of all things. The first proof is from creation: Allah is the creator of all things, and the creator of a thing without coercion must be willing it. The second proof is from the necessity of a specifying attribute: since all possibles are equally within divine power, the fact that some occur and others do not, and that they occur at specific times, requires a specifying factor, which is divine will. The commentary addresses an objection: that absolute creation does not necessarily imply will because it could be by necessitation. The response is that the proof relies on creation by power, and the negation of coercion after power suffices to prove will. The commentary also clarifies that this first proof reduces to the second, and that the second proof is the primary one. Another commentary addresses an objection that will is only a specifying factor for one's own actions, not for the actions of others. The answer is that the specification in creation itself requires divine will, regardless of whether the actions are attributed to servants.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
قدرة (qudra)
مخصص (mukhassis)
مقدور (maqdūr)
إكراه (ikrāh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that creation alone implies will, without the condition of 'without coercion' or 'by power'.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that absolute creation could be by necessitation, so it is creation by power (voluntary) that proves will. The negation of coercion after establishing power suffices.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why is a specifying attribute necessary?
A) Because all possibles are within God's power and some are preferred over others in occurrence and time
B) Because God must create all possibles
C) Because servants cannot create their own actions
D) Because will is only for one's own actions

Key Takeaway
The existence of creation and the specificity of the occurrence of possibles both necessitate the attribute of will in Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life — including your own choices, successes, and failures — is under Allah's will? How does this belief affect your reliance on Him in your daily decisions? Will you consciously submit to His specification even when it contradicts your own plans?
?
If the will of Allah is the ultimate specifier of all that occurs, what does that mean for your sense of control and autonomy? Are you willing to hold onto your plans lightly, ready to accept His specification with contentment? This week, identify one area where you struggle to accept Allah's will and make a sincere effort to surrender it to Him.
?
Reflect on the objection that will only applies to the agent's own actions. Do you sometimes act as if your actions are independent of Allah's will? How can the response in this passage — that Allah's will governs the very existence and timing of all things — reshape your understanding of your own responsibility and dependence on Him? Commit to seeking His will in your actions sincerely.

The Rational Argument That Allah Cannot Will the Impossible
Sanusi presents the rational argument against willing the impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that Allah does not will what does not happen, based on His knowledge and the impossibility of willing the impossible.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge makes the impossible unwillable.

This lesson presents the core rational argument used in Islamic theology to show that Allah's will is always consistent with His knowledge. Since Allah knows everything, He cannot will what He knows will not happen, because that would entail either contradiction or incapacity. We will examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
2
Identify the premise
The argument begins with Allah's knowledge that the disbeliever will not believe. Notice how this premise is used to derive the impossibility of belief.
3
Understand the two consequences
The author presents two possible outcomes if Allah willed the impossible: either it occurs (leading to change in knowledge) or it does not (leading to incapacity). Both are impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Grasp the specifying attribute
The author argues that no specifying attribute can be conceived for an impossible event because one side is impossible and the other necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير مريد لما لا يكون فلأنه تعالى علم من الكافر مثلاً أنه لا يؤمن فكان الإيمان محالاً لامتناع أن ينقلب العلم جهلاً، والله تعالى عالم باستحالته والعالم باستحالة الشيء لا يريده بالضرورة، وأيضاً لو أراده فإما أن يقع فيلزم الانقلاب أولا فيلزم عجزه وقصوره عن تحقيق مراده ) ولأنه لا يتصور منه أي من العالم باستحالة الشيء صفة مرجحة لأحد طرفيه لأن أحدهما مستحيل والآخر واجب فلا معنى لترجيح الصفة، وفيه بحث لأن عدم إيمان الكافر مراد الله مع كونه واجباً وأيضاً هو منقوض بما علم الله وجوده كإيمان المؤمن فإن أحد طرفيه واجب والآخر ممتنع فلا لترجيح الصفة، ويعضد هذا الذي هو مذهبنا إجماع السلف والخلف في الأعصار والأمصار على إطلاق قولهم ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن فإن هذا مروي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وقد تلقته الأمة بالقبول فيصح أن يكون مؤيداً، بل ربما يحتج به أيضاً، وإنما صرح بالإطلاق دفعاً لتوهم التقييد بأفعاله تعالى أو بما ليس من أفعال العباد الاختيارية، كما تأوله به المعتزلة ويدفع هذا التوهم أنهم كانوا يوردون كلامهم في معرض تعظيم الله وإعلاء شأنه والأول وهو ما شاء الله كان دليل الثاني وهو أنه تعالى غير مريد لما لا يكون، وذلك لأنه ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا : كل ما لم يكن لم يشأ الله . والثاني أعني ما يشأ لم يكن دليل الأول لانعكاسه بذلك الطريق إلى قولنا كل ما كان فقد شاء الله. قوله : ( وفيه بحث لأن عدم إيمان الكافر إلخ) فيه بحث لأن هذا إنما يرد على توجيه كلام المصنف في حمل أحد الطرفين فيه على الأحد الغير المعين، وأما لو حمل على أحدهما معيناً أعني الطرف المستحيل فلا إذ عدم تصور الصفة المرجحة في الطرف الممتنع ظاهر بخلافه في الطرف الواجب فإن الوجوب أثر العلم والعلم يتبع الوقوع والوقوع أثر الإرادة القديمة فليتأمل . قوله : ( في معرض تعظيم الله تعالى لا يخفى أن هذا كلام خطابي إذ إيجاد الأجرام العظام من السموات والأرضين وما بينها من الذوات بل كل ما دخل تحت الإمكان غير الحركات الجزئية والأعراض البشرية المحسوسة أمر عظيم فيه تعظيم وإعلاء شأن . قوله : وذلك لأنه ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلخ) فيه بحث لأن انعكاس الكلية كنفسها على طريقة القدماء مردود عند المتأخرين وهذا الاستدلال مبني عليه فإن قلت: مردودتيه باعتبار عدم كليته لا ينافي الاستدلال به في المادة الجزئية قلت : هذا حق في نفسه لكن ينافي ذكره في المقصد الرابع من المرصد الثالث في أقسام العلم حيث رد قول المدعي فيه بعد انعكاس الكلية كنفسها وقد أشير إلى هذا هناك أيضاً.
He is not willing for what does not come to be, because He, exalted, knows of the disbeliever, for example, that he does not believe, so belief would be impossible due to the impossibility that knowledge changes into ignorance. And God, exalted, knows its impossibility, and one who knows something is impossible does not will it necessarily. Also, if He willed it, either it would occur, leading to change [in knowledge], or not occur, leading to His incapacity and failure to achieve His will. And because one who knows something is impossible cannot conceive of a specifying attribute for one of its two sides, because one side is impossible and the other necessary, so there is no meaning to specification. There is discussion in it because the non-belief of the disbeliever is willed by God while it is necessary, and also it is disproved by what God knows will exist, like the belief of the believer, for one of its sides is necessary and the other impossible, so there is no specification. And this — which is our doctrine — is supported by the consensus of the early and later generations in all ages and lands on their absolute saying: 'What God wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be.' Indeed, this is narrated from the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, and the ummah has accepted it, so it can be a supporter; rather, it is also used as evidence. And they explicitly stated it absolutely to repel the suspicion of limitation to His acts or to what is not among the voluntary acts of servants, as the Mu'tazila interpreted it. And this suspicion is repelled by the fact that they used to utter their words in the context of glorifying God and exalting His status. And the first — that which God wills comes to be — is evidence for the second — that He is not willing for what does not come to be — because it inverts by contrapositive to our saying: everything that does not come to be, God did not will it. And the second — that which He does not will does not come to be — is evidence for the first, by its inversion in that way to our saying: everything that comes to be, God has willed it. His statement: 'And there is discussion in it because the non-belief of the disbeliever...' — there is discussion in it because this only objects to the author's interpretation of 'one of the two sides' as the indefinite non-specific one. But if it is interpreted as a specific one, i.e., the impossible side, then [the objection falls] because the lack of conception of a specifying attribute for the impossible side is apparent, unlike the necessary side, for necessity is an effect of knowledge, and knowledge follows occurrence, and occurrence is an effect of eternal will, so let it be considered. His statement: 'In the context of glorifying God, exalted' — it is not hidden that this is rhetorical speech, for creating the great bodies of the heavens and earth and the essences between them, indeed everything that falls under possibility, other than partial movements and sensible human accidents, is a great matter, in which there is glorification and exaltation of status. His statement: 'Because it inverts by contrapositive, etc.' — there is discussion in it because the inversion of a universal proposition as itself, according to the method of the ancients, is rejected by the later scholars, and this reasoning is based on it. If you say: its rejection due to its lack of universality does not preclude reasoning with it in particular instances, I say: this is true in itself, but it contradicts its mention in the fourth objective of the third observatory on the divisions of knowledge, where he rejected the claim of the claimant after the inversion of the universal as itself, and it was pointed to there as well.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the argument that Allah does not will what does not happen, based on His knowledge. Since Allah knows the disbeliever will not believe, belief becomes impossible, and one who knows impossibility cannot will it. If He willed it, either it would happen (leading to change in knowledge) or not (showing incapacity). Also, no specifying attribute is conceivable for an impossible opposite. However, there is a discussion (فيه بحث) because the non-belief of the disbeliever is willed by Allah while being necessary (since it is known), and this argument is contradicted by the case of the believer's belief, where one side is necessary and the other impossible. The text then cites the famous hadith 'What Allah wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be' as a support from consensus and prophetic tradition. The absolute wording rejects Mu'tazilite interpretations that limit will to certain acts. The logical inversion of the hadith is presented to prove both directions: if something occurs, it must be willed; if it does not occur, it was not willed. The commentary then addresses each point: the 'discussion' is limited to a specific interpretation of 'one of the two sides'; the hadith is a rhetorical statement of glorification; and the logical inversion is based on the method of the ancients which later scholars rejected, but can still be used in particular instances, though there is inconsistency with an earlier part of the work.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
علم (ʿilm)
استحالة (istiḥāla)
عكس النقيض (ʿaks al-naqīḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Allah can will the impossible if He so chooses, as His power is absolute.
Correction: The argument shows that willing the impossible is rationally contradictory because it would either change His knowledge or prove incapacity, both impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't Allah will what does not happen?
A) Because He is too busy
B) Because He knows it is impossible and willing the impossible leads to contradiction
C) Because He only wills good things
D) Because the disbeliever's will is stronger

Key Takeaway
Allah's will is necessarily aligned with His knowledge; He cannot will what He knows will not happen because it would entail logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument establishes that Allah's will is bound by logic and knowledge. Have you ever harbored the thought that Allah could act against His own knowledge or change His mind? How does this affect your reliance on His promises and threats?
?
If Allah cannot will what is impossible, then every event that occurs is exactly what He knew and willed. Do you truly accept this even for the painful events in your life? Or do you secretly wish He had willed otherwise?
?
The proof shows that willing the impossible would imply either a change in knowledge or incapacity—both impossible for Allah. Does your trust in Allah's wisdom and power withstand this logical scrutiny? Will you commit to submitting to His decree without reservation?

The Hadith Evidence That Allah's Will Is All-Encompassing
The passage cites the hadith and its logical inversion as evidence for divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith 'What Allah wills comes to be' and its logical inversion support the doctrine that everything that occurs is willed by Allah.

Core Idea
What occurs is willed; what does not occur is not willed.

This lesson explores the famous hadith 'What Allah wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be.' It is cited as a consensus-based proof for the Ash'ari doctrine of divine will. The passage also discusses a logical inversion of the hadith that proves both directions. We'll also examine the critique and responses that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from discussion to end
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وفيه بحث' to the end. Then read the translation. Notice how the author first addresses an objection, then introduces the hadith.
2
Understand the discussion about the rational argument
The discussion points out that the earlier argument seems to conflict with the fact that Allah wills the disbeliever's non-belief while it is necessary. Notice how the commentator resolves this by distinguishing between indefinite and specific sides.
3
Identify the hadith and its authority
The hadith 'ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن' is presented as a support from consensus and prophetic tradition. Note the claim that it is narrated from the Prophet and accepted by the ummah.
4
Grasp the logical inversion
The author shows how the first part of the hadith inverts to 'كل ما لم يكن لم يشأ الله' and the second to 'كل ما كان فقد شاء الله'. This establishes the two directions.
This is an example of contrapositive (عكس النقيض).
5
Examine the commentary on the inversion
The commentator notes that the inversion method used is based on ancient logic, which later scholars rejected for universal propositions. However, it can still be used in particular instances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير مريد لما لا يكون فلأنه تعالى علم من الكافر مثلاً أنه لا يؤمن فكان الإيمان محالاً لامتناع أن ينقلب العلم جهلاً، والله تعالى عالم باستحالته والعالم باستحالة الشيء لا يريده بالضرورة، وأيضاً لو أراده فإما أن يقع فيلزم الانقلاب أولا فيلزم عجزه وقصوره عن تحقيق مراده ) ولأنه لا يتصور منه أي من العالم باستحالة الشيء صفة مرجحة لأحد طرفيه لأن أحدهما مستحيل والآخر واجب فلا معنى لترجيح الصفة، وفيه بحث لأن عدم إيمان الكافر مراد الله مع كونه واجباً وأيضاً هو منقوض بما علم الله وجوده كإيمان المؤمن فإن أحد طرفيه واجب والآخر ممتنع فلا لترجيح الصفة، ويعضد هذا الذي هو مذهبنا إجماع السلف والخلف في الأعصار والأمصار على إطلاق قولهم ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن فإن هذا مروي عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وقد تلقته الأمة بالقبول فيصح أن يكون مؤيداً، بل ربما يحتج به أيضاً، وإنما صرح بالإطلاق دفعاً لتوهم التقييد بأفعاله تعالى أو بما ليس من أفعال العباد الاختيارية، كما تأوله به المعتزلة ويدفع هذا التوهم أنهم كانوا يوردون كلامهم في معرض تعظيم الله وإعلاء شأنه والأول وهو ما شاء الله كان دليل الثاني وهو أنه تعالى غير مريد لما لا يكون، وذلك لأنه ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا : كل ما لم يكن لم يشأ الله . والثاني أعني ما يشأ لم يكن دليل الأول لانعكاسه بذلك الطريق إلى قولنا كل ما كان فقد شاء الله. قوله : ( وفيه بحث لأن عدم إيمان الكافر إلخ) فيه بحث لأن هذا إنما يرد على توجيه كلام المصنف في حمل أحد الطرفين فيه على الأحد الغير المعين، وأما لو حمل على أحدهما معيناً أعني الطرف المستحيل فلا إذ عدم تصور الصفة المرجحة في الطرف الممتنع ظاهر بخلافه في الطرف الواجب فإن الوجوب أثر العلم والعلم يتبع الوقوع والوقوع أثر الإرادة القديمة فليتأمل . قوله : ( في معرض تعظيم الله تعالى لا يخفى أن هذا كلام خطابي إذ إيجاد الأجرام العظام من السموات والأرضين وما بينها من الذوات بل كل ما دخل تحت الإمكان غير الحركات الجزئية والأعراض البشرية المحسوسة أمر عظيم فيه تعظيم وإعلاء شأن . قوله : وذلك لأنه ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلخ) فيه بحث لأن انعكاس الكلية كنفسها على طريقة القدماء مردود عند المتأخرين وهذا الاستدلال مبني عليه فإن قلت: مردودتيه باعتبار عدم كليته لا ينافي الاستدلال به في المادة الجزئية قلت : هذا حق في نفسه لكن ينافي ذكره في المقصد الرابع من المرصد الثالث في أقسام العلم حيث رد قول المدعي فيه بعد انعكاس الكلية كنفسها وقد أشير إلى هذا هناك أيضاً.
He is not willing for what does not come to be, because He, exalted, knows of the disbeliever, for example, that he does not believe, so belief would be impossible due to the impossibility that knowledge changes into ignorance. And God, exalted, knows its impossibility, and one who knows something is impossible does not will it necessarily. Also, if He willed it, either it would occur, leading to change [in knowledge], or not occur, leading to His incapacity and failure to achieve His will. And because one who knows something is impossible cannot conceive of a specifying attribute for one of its two sides, because one side is impossible and the other necessary, so there is no meaning to specification. There is discussion in it because the non-belief of the disbeliever is willed by God while it is necessary, and also it is disproved by what God knows will exist, like the belief of the believer, for one of its sides is necessary and the other impossible, so there is no specification. And this — which is our doctrine — is supported by the consensus of the early and later generations in all ages and lands on their absolute saying: 'What God wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be.' Indeed, this is narrated from the Prophet, may God bless him and grant him peace, and the ummah has accepted it, so it can be a supporter; rather, it is also used as evidence. And they explicitly stated it absolutely to repel the suspicion of limitation to His acts or to what is not among the voluntary acts of servants, as the Mu'tazila interpreted it. And this suspicion is repelled by the fact that they used to utter their words in the context of glorifying God and exalting His status. And the first — that which God wills comes to be — is evidence for the second — that He is not willing for what does not come to be — because it inverts by contrapositive to our saying: everything that does not come to be, God did not will it. And the second — that which He does not will does not come to be — is evidence for the first, by its inversion in that way to our saying: everything that comes to be, God has willed it. His statement: 'And there is discussion in it because the non-belief of the disbeliever...' — there is discussion in it because this only objects to the author's interpretation of 'one of the two sides' as the indefinite non-specific one. But if it is interpreted as a specific one, i.e., the impossible side, then [the objection falls] because the lack of conception of a specifying attribute for the impossible side is apparent, unlike the necessary side, for necessity is an effect of knowledge, and knowledge follows occurrence, and occurrence is an effect of eternal will, so let it be considered. His statement: 'In the context of glorifying God, exalted' — it is not hidden that this is rhetorical speech, for creating the great bodies of the heavens and earth and the essences between them, indeed everything that falls under possibility, other than partial movements and sensible human accidents, is a great matter, in which there is glorification and exaltation of status. His statement: 'Because it inverts by contrapositive, etc.' — there is discussion in it because the inversion of a universal proposition as itself, according to the method of the ancients, is rejected by the later scholars, and this reasoning is based on it. If you say: its rejection due to its lack of universality does not preclude reasoning with it in particular instances, I say: this is true in itself, but it contradicts its mention in the fourth objective of the third observatory on the divisions of knowledge, where he rejected the claim of the claimant after the inversion of the universal as itself, and it was pointed to there as well.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the argument that Allah does not will what does not happen, based on His knowledge. Since Allah knows the disbeliever will not believe, belief becomes impossible, and one who knows impossibility cannot will it. If He willed it, either it would happen (leading to change in knowledge) or not (showing incapacity). Also, no specifying attribute is conceivable for an impossible opposite. However, there is a discussion (فيه بحث) because the non-belief of the disbeliever is willed by Allah while being necessary (since it is known), and this argument is contradicted by the case of the believer's belief, where one side is necessary and the other impossible. The text then cites the famous hadith 'What Allah wills comes to be, and what He does not will does not come to be' as a support from consensus and prophetic tradition. The absolute wording rejects Mu'tazilite interpretations that limit will to certain acts. The logical inversion of the hadith is presented to prove both directions: if something occurs, it must be willed; if it does not occur, it was not willed. The commentary then addresses each point: the 'discussion' is limited to a specific interpretation of 'one of the two sides'; the hadith is a rhetorical statement of glorification; and the logical inversion is based on the method of the ancients which later scholars rejected, but can still be used in particular instances, though there is inconsistency with an earlier part of the work.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda)
عكس النقيض (ʿaks al-naqīḍ)
إجماع (ijmāʿ)
السلف والخلف (al-salaf wa al-khalaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the hadith only applies to Allah's own actions, not to human voluntary acts.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the absolute wording is meant to reject such limitation, and that the hadith covers all occurrences.

Checkpoint
What does the logical inversion of the hadith's second part (ما لم يشأ لم يكن) prove?
A) That everything that occurs was willed by Allah
B) That Allah only wills good things
C) That Allah does not will the actions of servants
D) That the hadith is not authentic

Key Takeaway
The hadith and its contrapositive establish that everything that occurs is willed by Allah, and everything that does not occur is not willed by Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something painful or disappointing happens in your life, do you truly believe that it is willed by Allah? Or do you find yourself thinking it shouldn't have happened? How can you train your heart to say 'What Allah wills' even in those moments?
?
If you fully internalized that nothing occurs except by Allah's will, would your worry about the future decrease? What is one specific worry you can surrender to Him starting today?
?
The hadith says 'What Allah wills comes to be.' Is there an area of your life where you are resisting His will—perhaps by discontentment, rebellion, or despair? What would it look like to accept His will in that area right now?

God's Command Contrary to His Will Is Not Foolish
First Mu'tazilite objection and three-part response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite objection against God willing all things, and the three rational responses that show commanding contrary to will is not inherently foolish.

Core Idea
Commanding contrary to will is not foolish when the purpose is not the act itself.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God wills disbelief, then commanding faith would be foolish. The response demonstrates three scenarios where commanding contrary to will is rational and not foolish. These scenarios help us see that divine commands can have purposes beyond the commanded act.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the response's main principle
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the Mu'tazilite claim and the initial rebuttal that restricts foolishness to commands whose sole purpose is the act itself.
Notice the phrase 'الغرض من الأمر منحصراً' – the key condition.
2
Identify the three scenarios
List the three 'وجوه' (aspects): test of a servant, king's excuse, and compulsion. See how each illustrates a command contrary to will without foolishness.
Each scenario has a different purpose: testing, providing an excuse, or necessity.
3
Consider the objection about form vs reality
Note the objection that the second scenario is only a 'form of command' not real command. Read the answer that a rational person may command if he knows the act won't occur and there is benefit in the form.
This preserves the reality of the command while acknowledging the benefit.
4
Reflect on how this preserves God's wisdom
Think about how these examples show that God's command of faith despite willing disbelief for some is not foolish. The purpose is to test, provide excuse, or manifest justice.
This defends divine wisdom without denying God's will over all things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجوا أي المعتزلة على أنه تعالى لا يريد الكفر والمعاصي بوجوه عقلية : الأول : لو كان تعالى مريداً لكفر الكافر وقد أمره بالإيمان فالأمر بخلاف مايريده يعد عند العقلاء سفيهاً فيلزم السفه في أحكام الله تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً، قلنا: لانسلم أن الآمر بخلاف ما يريده يعد سفيهاً وإنما يكون كذلك لو كان الغرض من الأمر منحصراً في إيقاع المأمور به يوضحه وجوه ثلاثة : الأول : إن الممتحن لعبده هل يطيعه أم لا قد يأمره ولا يريد منه الفعل، أما الأول وهو أن الصادر منه أمر حقيقة فلأنه إذا أتى العبد بالفعل يقال : امتثل أمر سيده وأما الثاني : وهو أنه لا يريد الفعل منه يحصل مقصوده وهو الامتحان أطاع أو عصى فلاسفه في الأمر بما لا يريده الأمر. الثاني : إنه إذا عاتب الملك ضارب عبده فاعتذر بعصيانه والملك يتوعده بالقتل إن لم يظهر عصيانه فإنه يأمره بفعل تمهيداً لعذره، ويرد عصيانه فيه، فإن أحداً لا يريد ما يفضي إلى قتله بل ما يخلصه عنه فقد أمر بخلاف ما يريده ولا سفه فإن قيل : الموجود هاهنا صورة الأمر لا حقيقته فإن العاقل لا يأمر بما يؤدي حصوله إلى هلاكه . أجيب : بأنه قد يأمر به إذا علم أنه لا يحصل وكان في الأمر به فائدة بخلاف الإرادة فإنها لا تتعلق به أصلاً . الثالث : إن الملجأ إلى الأمر قد يأمر ولا يريد فعل المأمور به بل يريد خلافه ولا يعد سفيهاً
They argued—i.e., the Muʿtazila—that He, exalted, does not will disbelief and sins through rational arguments. The first: If God, exalted, were willing the disbelief of the disbeliever and commanded him to believe, then commanding contrary to what He wills would be considered foolish (safīh) by the rational people, so foolishness would be necessary in God's rulings—God is exalted above that greatly. We say: We do not concede that the one who commands contrary to what He wills is considered foolish. Rather, he would be so only if the purpose of the command is restricted to effecting the commanded thing. This is clarified by three aspects: The first: The one who tests his slave whether he obeys him or not may command him and not intend the act from him. As for the first—that what issues from him is truly a command—because when the slave performs the act, it is said: he complied with his master's command. And as for the second—that he does not intend the act from him—his purpose (which is the test) is achieved whether he obeys or disobeys, so there is no foolishness in commanding what he does not intend. The second: If a king rebukes his slave for striking someone, and the slave excuses himself by his disobedience, and the king threatens him with death if he does not show his disobedience, then he commands him to commit an act as a pretext for his excuse, and he intends his disobedience in it. For no one intends what leads to his death, but rather what saves him from it. He commanded contrary to what he wills, and there is no foolishness. If it is said: What exists here is the form of the command, not its reality, for a rational person does not command what would lead to his destruction if it occurs, the answer is: He may command it if he knows it will not occur, and there is benefit in the form of command, unlike will, which does not relate to it at all. The third: One who is compelled to command may command and not intend the act commanded, but rather intend its opposite, and he is not considered foolish.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite objection against the doctrine that God wills all beings, including disbelief. The objection is: If God wills disbelief and also commands faith, then He is commanding contrary to His will, which rational people consider foolish—this would apply to God, which is impossible. The response has three points. First, a master testing his slave may issue a genuine command but not actually intend the slave to perform the act; the purpose is to test obedience. Thus commanding without intending the act is not foolish. Second, a king commands a slave to commit a wrong to provide an excuse, intending the slave's disobedience to save his life. Although the command is contrary to his will, it is not foolish because the purpose is the excuse, not the act. An objection that this is only the form of command is answered: a rational person may command knowing the act will not occur, and there is benefit in the form. Third, a person compelled to issue a command may command something while intending its opposite without being foolish. These three cases show that commanding contrary to will is not inherently foolish.

Key Terms
مريد
سفه
الأمر بخلاف الإرادة
الغرض من الأمر
امتحان
صورة الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Commanding contrary to one's will is always foolish.
Correction: It is only foolish if the sole purpose of the command is to effect the commanded act. In many cases, there are other legitimate purposes (testing, providing an excuse, compulsion) that make such commands rational.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the first objection, when is commanding contrary to one's will not considered foolish?
A) When the command is issued in anger.
B) When the purpose of the command is not restricted to the commanded act itself.
C) When the commander is not rational.
D) When the commanded act is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first Mu'tazilite objection fails because commanding contrary to will is not inherently foolish; it is only foolish if the command's purpose is solely to effect the commanded act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command from God that conflicts with your desires, do you instinctively label it as unreasonable? Before you dismiss it, will you pause to ask, 'What higher purpose might God have for this command in my life?'
?
The second example shows a king commanding something to save his slave. Can you recall a time when a difficult command from God actually saved you from a greater harm? How might this memory change your response to His commands today?
?
If God's commands are for your ultimate good even when they seem contrary to your will, what is holding you back from full obedience? Will you commit today to trust His wisdom over your own feelings, especially in the areas you find most challenging?

The Second Mu'tazilite Objection: Would Willed Disbelief Be Obedience?
Second Mu'tazilite objection and response distinguishing command from will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite objection that if God wills disbelief, it would be obedience, and the response that obedience is conformity to command, not to will.

Core Idea
Obedience follows command, not will — a key distinction in Islamic theology.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if God wills all things, including disbelief, then the disbeliever's act would be in conformity to God's will, making it an act of obedience — a clear absurdity. This lesson examines the objection and the critical response that distinguishes between God's command and His will.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the Mu'tazilite reasoning that if disbelief is willed by God, it conforms to His will and would therefore be obedience — which is impossible.
Notice the structure: they present a conditional argument leading to a contradiction.
2
Analyze the response
The response defines obedience as conformity to God's command, not His will. Command and will are separate attributes; they can diverge.
Recall earlier lessons where command exists without will (e.g., tests).
3
Examine al-Amidi's analogy
Al-Amidi provides a counterexample: if A wills something from B and B does it unknowingly, B is not obedient to A. This proves conformity to will alone is insufficient.
Consider how this analogy strengthens the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : من وجوه استدلالاتهم لو كان الكفار مراد الله تعالى لكان فعله والإتيان به موافقة لمراد الله تعالى فيكون طاعة به وإنه باطل ضرورة من الدين قلنا : الطاعة موافقة الأمر، والأمر غير الإرادة وغير مستلزم لها لانفكاكها عنه في الصور المذكورة، قال الآمدي : ويدل على أن موافقة الإرادة ليست طاعة أنه لو أراد شخص شيئاً من آخر فوقع المراد من الآخر على وفق إرادة المريد ولا شعور للفاعل بإرادته فإنه لا يعد منه طاعة له كيف والإرادة مثاباً
The second of their argumentative aspects: If the disbelievers were willed by God, then their act and performance of it would be conformity to the will (murād) of God, so it would be obedience (ṭāʿa) by it, and that is false by necessity of religion. We say: Obedience is conformity to the command (amr), and the command is other than the will and does not necessitate it, because they are separable in the mentioned cases. Al-Āmidī said: Evidence that conformity to will is not obedience is that if a person wills something from another, and the willed thing occurs from the other in accordance with the willer's will and the agent has no awareness of his will, then it is not considered obedience from him. How could it be, while the will is [؟] reward?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the second Mu'tazilite objection: If God wills disbelief, then the disbeliever's act of disbelief conforms to God's will, making it an act of obedience—which is impossible because disbelief is a sin. The response distinguishes between command and will. Obedience is defined as conformity to God's command, not to His will. They are separable, as seen in previous examples. The supporting argument from al-Amidī: If A wills something and B does it without knowing A's will, B is not obedient to A. Thus conformity to will alone does not constitute obedience. The last phrase 'كيف والإرادة مثاباً' appears truncated, possibly meaning 'how could it be while the will is [related to reward]?'

Key Terms
طاعة
موافقة الأمر
موافقة الإرادة
الأمر غير الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whatever God wills is necessarily commanded and therefore obedience.
Correction: Obedience is specifically conformity to God's command, which can differ from His will. Disbelief is willed but not commanded, so it is not obedience.

Checkpoint
According to the response, how is obedience (ṭāʿa) defined?
A) Conformity to God's will.
B) Conformity to God's command.
C) Conformity to both command and will.
D) Performing an act with knowledge of God's will.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite objection fails because obedience is defined by command, not will, and these two divine attributes are separable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you measure your obedience by how well you follow God's commands, or do you assume that whatever happens is God's will and therefore acceptable? How often do you actively seek out the command rather than passively accepting events?
?
Think of a time when you did something you knew was against God's clear command, but it turned out 'well' or felt destined. Did you treat that outcome as obedience because it happened? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
Al-Āmidī's analogy shows that unawareness of the willer's will means no obedience. Are you truly aware of what God commands you, or do you live in ignorance? What specific step will you take today to align your actions with His command, not just with what occurs?
